In the reference week, typical normal days for this customer never drop below ~98–100 except when zeros appear (Day 3). The scattered low values and irregular pattern indicate that each sample was modified independently, consistent with a per-sample random multiplier attack. Attack 4 was considered but ruled out because the reduced readings are not restricted to a single continuous block. Attack 1 was also considered but rejected because a constant multiplier would not produce a minimum as low as 20.62 while retaining a maximum of 181.66 (the ratio would be too extreme for a single scaling factor).

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week: Each original 15‑minute reading is multiplied by a different random factor between 0 and 1. This creates a profile where some readings are only slightly reduced while others are drastically lowered, yielding a wide and erratic spread of values.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: Mean consumption drops from ~190 (normal Tuesday) to ~101. Magnitude is unevenly reduced, and the typical morning‑to‑afternoon consumption ramp and evening decline are severely distorted. The trend is no longer representative of genuine usage.
- Possible technical causes: Meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, or false data injection attacks that alter meter outputs in a random, non‑uniform manner across the day.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Reasoning content 
We need to determine if the query day (DAY_ID=2) is original or manipulated, and if manipulated, identify the attack type.

First, analyze the reference week data. The reference week includes 7 days (Day 0 to Day 6). The query day is also DAY_ID=2 but with dom=16 (day of month), while reference Day 2 had dom=2. The dow (day of week) is 1 (Tuesday) for both. So it's a Tuesday, but a different week (dom=16 vs dom=2). The season is summer (August). The reference week shows the customer's typical consumption over a week. We need to compare the query day's pattern against the typical pattern for that day of week, and also overall typical behavior.

Look at the per-day statistics:
Reference week:
Day 0: min=100.73, max=115.42, std=3.45, mean=109.78 (low variation, mostly constant around 110)
Day 1: min=111.23, max=280.17, std=60.15, mean=195.21 (high variation, typical high-consumption day)
Day 2: min=104.93, max=281.22, std=66.15, mean=190.32 (similar high variation)
Day 3: min=0.00, max=172.09, std=30.90, mean=100.50 (zero values appear! Actually min=0.00, indicating some zero readings, mean lower, some low-consumption pattern)
Day 4: min=100.73, max=275.97, std=65.56, mean=184.04 (high variation again)
Day 5: min=98.64, max=275.97, std=65.30, mean=183.89 (high variation)
Day 6: min=99.69, max=121.72, std=4.97, mean=109.35 (low variation, like Day 0)

Query day: min=20.62, max=181.66, std=44.32, mean=101.05.

Observations: The query day's min is 20.62, which is far lower than any non-zero min in the reference week except Day 3 which had zero values. The max is 181.66, which is lower than the typical high-consumption days (which had max around 275-281). The mean is 101.05, which is similar to Day 3's mean (100.50) and Day 0/6 (around 109-110). The std is 44.32, indicating significant variation but less than the high-consumption days (std ~60) and more than the low-variation days (std ~3-5).

Now, we need to look at the actual consumption profiles. Since the query day is a Tuesday (dow=1), the comparable reference days might be Day 2 (Tuesday of the reference week) and also perhaps other weekdays. But we must consider that the customer might have different patterns across weeks. In the reference week, Day 2 (Tuesday) had a pattern: it started low (around 110) in the early morning, then around reading index 22 (around 5:30am) it started rising, peaked around 1pm-3pm (readings 52-60) with values around 250-280, then dropped in the evening. This pattern is similar to Day 1, Day 4, Day 5, while Day 0, Day 3, Day 6 are different (Day 3 had a weird drop to zero in the middle). So the typical workday pattern seems to be high consumption during daytime. The query day's max is 181.66, significantly lower. Also the min is 20.62, which is much lower than any minimum in the reference workdays (min around 100-105). So the query day seems to have a lower magnitude overall.

Looking at the raw query day series: it has many low values (like 20.62, 22.31, 23.22, 26.00, 25.42, etc.) interspersed with higher values. The pattern doesn't show a smooth typical shape. There are many values below 50, which are abnormally low for this customer. In the reference week, except Day 3 (which had zeros), the lowest values are around 98-100. So these sub-50 values are anomalous. This suggests manipulation.

What attack could produce such a pattern? It might be an attack that zeros or reduces some intervals but not others, like Attack 2 (on-off), Attack 4 (random period reduction), Attack 6 (cut-off), Attack 7 (cut-off subtract), or Attack IEEE (zeroing some intervals). The query day has some very low values but not actually zero; the min is 20.62, not zero. So it's not completely zeroed out. However, some attacks like Attack 2 and IEEE involve replacing with zero, but here we see low numbers, not zero. Attack 1 multiplies by constant factor (0-1), which would reduce uniformly but keep the shape; if the factor is constant, the relative shape remains. The query day's min to max ratio: min 20.62 vs max 181.66, which is a ratio of about 0.11 if the original min was ~100, but we need to see if it's a uniform reduction. If we assume the original day had a typical profile like Day 2, with values ranging ~100 to 280, and if we multiply by a factor around 0.5, we'd get 50-140, but the query day max is 181.66, which is still high. The mean of query day is ~101, while Day 2 reference mean is 190. So the factor would be about 0.53 if it were a uniform multiplication. But then the min would be 104.93 * 0.53 ≈ 55.6, which is close to the observed minimum of 20.62? Not exactly, but there is a low value of 20.62. So perhaps not uniform.

Attack 3 multiplies by different random factors per sample, which could yield such a scattered pattern with some very low values. Attack 4 reduces a random period; if that period includes reductions but not necessarily zeros, it could produce low values. Attack 5 replaces each sample with a false value from average consumption * random (0,1). That would yield values independent of original, so the pattern would be completely random; the query day has some structure-like higher values around 150-180, and lower values interspersed. It might match Attack 5. Attack 6 sets a cut-off, so values above a threshold are clipped; that would produce a maximum cap. Here max is 181.66; if the original max is ~280, a cutoff would produce many values exactly at the cutoff, but we don't see many repeated identical values at 181.66; only one reading is 181.66. So not likely. Attack 7 subtracts a cutoff, replacing negatives with zero; that would produce zeros, not low positive values. Attack 12 replaces with a lower-consuming user's profile; that would give a completely different shape, maybe with many low values, but the pattern might still be a real consumption shape, just lower. The query day has a min of 20.62, which is plausible for another user but not this one. Attack IEEE: for each 15-min interval, either real or zero; again, zeros would appear. The query day has no zeros, so not that.

So the most plausible attacks are those that reduce but not to zero: Attack 1 (constant multiplier) but the min is too low relative to max if constant. Attack 3 (random multipliers per sample) could produce a wide range of reduced values, including very low ones. Attack 4 (random period reduction) could also produce a period of low values. Let's examine the query day series more closely. The series: 
64.06, 108.00, 150.68, 125.64, 22.31, 37.64, 118.18, 41.17, 64.65, 85.76, 57.52, 153.50, 96.57, 149.57, 134.77, 138.97, 52.01, 20.69, 67.37, 155.50, 63.37, 102.35, 53.52, 23.22, 94.65, 128.83, 112.89, 86.33, 144.55, 138.36, 87.56, 127.77, 162.90, 92.61, 143.41, 74.78, 91.88, 98.43, 164.95, 157.14, 154.24, 170.94, 117.78, 141.30, 38.89, 51.76, 73.85, 87.98, 35.54, 161.28, 151.37, 124.16, 154.43, 41.50, 159.11, 154.62, 84.36, 38.86, 113.01, 85.49, 65.77, 37.40, 99.61, 145.52, 144.36, 23.06, 71.33, 143.68, 54.77, 127.32, 78.86, 76.62, 86.96, 119.27, 140.62, 99.85, 44.42, 20.62, 157.21, 116.79, 170.81, 26.00, 101.00, 119.41, 92.65, 95.26, 181.66, 144.54, 104.68, 133.77, 25.42, 44.01, 71.28, 146.47, 102.28, 153.26

There are many low values (20s, 30s, 40s) interspersed with higher values. This looks like a pattern where some readings are dramatically reduced, possibly randomly. Attack 3 (per-sample random factor) would multiply each original sample by a different factor ∈ (0,1). If the original profile had values ~100-280, then a factor of 0.1 to 0.8 could yield values like 10 to 224. But here max is 181.66, so some high original values around 280 multiplied by ~0.65 would be 182, consistent. Low original values ~100 multiplied by 0.2 would be 20. So Attack 3 fits.

Attack 4: a random period where all measurements are reduced. That would produce a contiguous block of reduced values, not interspersed. The query day's low values are not contiguous; they appear scattered (e.g., low at index 4,5, then high, then low, etc.). So not a single contiguous period. So Attack 4 is unlikely.

Attack 5: each sample replaced by a value = average normal consumption * random(0,1). The average normal consumption for this customer? For Day 2 reference mean is 190.32, but overall the customer's daily means vary (some 109, some 190). If using the overall average daily consumption? Maybe they used the average across days? The normal weekly mean? Could be around 147? The query day mean is 101.05. If attack 5 uses average normal consumption, say 150, then each sample is 150 * r (0,1), yielding range 0-150, which doesn't match max 181.66. So likely not.

Attack 1 constant multiplier: if factor ~0.53, min would be ~55, but we have 20. So not constant.

Thus, the most likely attack is Attack 3: per-sample random multiplier. This attack changes both trend and magnitude, and is caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection.

Now, confidence: High, because the scattered low values and varying highs indicate random scaling per sample.

Alternative considered: Attack 4 (period reduction) but ruled out because low values are not contiguous. Attack 5 (random from average) ruled out because max exceeds average.

If we compare trends: In the reference day, there is a pattern of periods with values around 38-40 interspersed with dips. In the query day, we see similar pattern but scaled down and with deep dips. For example, early morning (first few intervals): reference has 28.51, 39.07, 39.07, 38.01, 39.07, 29.57, 30.62; query has 11.74, 31.32, 10.89, 6.35, 24.45, 14.12, 25.07. Not proportional. But note that in reference, the high values are around 39, low around 28-30. In query, high values are around 24-32, low values around 4-14. There is a dynamic range expansion: the ratio of max to min is larger. That suggests that the attack might be Attack 6 or 7? Attack 6: "A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value." That would cap values, not create low values. Attack 7: subtract a cut-off point from each sample, if negative, zero. That would reduce all values, and could create zeros if original value is below the cut-off. But here we don't see zeros, we see positive small values. Attack 3: multiply each sample by a different random factor between 0 and 1. That could produce the observed effect: if original values are 27-40, multiply by random factors from ~0.1 to 0.85, we'd get a range from ~2.7 to 34. That matches roughly (min 3.86, max 32.75). Moreover, the standard deviation would increase relative to the mean because the scaling factors are random, introducing extra variance. Indeed, reference std ~3.6, mean ~36.4; query std 8.45, mean 17.55; the coefficient of variation (std/mean) is 0.48 vs 0.1, so variability relative to mean is much higher. This is consistent with Attack 3's "unique factor per sample" causing both trend and amplitude changes.

Attack 4: reduces measurements over a randomly selected period. That would create a block of reduced values, not scattered like this. The query day has low values throughout the day. So not Attack 4.

Attack 5: each sample replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1. The average normal consumption is around 36.4 (mean of reference Day 4). So Attack 5 would produce values roughly between 0 and 36.4, but completely random with no correlation to the original pattern. Looking at the query day, there are still some patterns: e.g., there is a peak around 31-32 in several places (e.g., index 1: 31.32, index 32: 31.59, index 40: 32.15, index 46: 30.18, index 51: 25.34, index 54: 32.53, index 56: 32.75, index 61: 29.13, index 62: 28.33, index 74: 32.69, index 79: 31.50, index 83: 28.82). These appear at times that might correspond to high usage periods. In fact, if we align indices, we can see that the query day has peaks similar to the reference day's peaks but scaled. For instance, reference index 16: 40.13, query index 16: 6.06? No. Let's align carefully. Indices 0-95.

The ratios vary wildly: 0.10 to 0.93. This is exactly what Attack 3 does: multiplies by different random factors per sample. The resulting pattern changes trend and amplitude. Attack 1 would use a constant factor, so all ratios would be the same. Here they are not constant. So Attack 3 is the most likely.

Could it be Attack 4? Attack 4 reduces measurements over a randomly selected period. That would mean a contiguous block where values are lowered by some method (maybe subtraction or scaling). Looking at the query day, there is no single contiguous low block; lows are interspersed. For instance, there are lows at indices 3 (6.35), 8 (11.95), 12 (7.14), 16 (6.06), 19 (6.82), 21 (4.55), 22 (5.17), 25 (7.83), 28 (6.71), 30 (10.22), and so on, with highs in between. So not a single contiguous block. Attack 2 (on-off with zeros) would have zeros, not low positive values. Attack IEEE also uses zeros. Attack 5 would have no correlation with the original pattern; but here the high values often correspond to original high values (e.g., index 54: 32.53 vs original 39.07; index 56: 32.75 vs original 38.01; index 1: 31.32 vs 39.07; index 32: 31.59 vs 33.79; index 40: 32.15 vs 36.96; index 46: 30.18 vs 36.96; index 78: 32.69 vs 38.01; index 83: 31.50 vs 36.96). There is some alignment, though the ratios differ. This alignment suggests the original pattern is still somewhat present but heavily distorted. Attack 5 would generate values independent of original, so alignment would be coincidental. It's more likely that the attacker applied varying scaling factors, i.e., Attack 3.

Attack 6 (capping) would only lower high values, not create deep lows. Attack 7 (subtract constant) would create zeros for values below the constant, but here we have very low values but not zeros. If a constant was subtracted, say 25, then original values 27-40 would become 2-15, and high values up to 15, but we see highs up to 32. So subtraction can't explain high values. Only scaling can produce both deep lows and moderate highs from a high dynamic range original (27-40). Actually, if we subtract 20, original 27 becomes 7, 40 becomes 20. That would give range 7-20, but we see values above 20 up to 32. If we subtract 10, range 17-30, but we see values down to 3.86. So subtraction can't match both extremes. Addition of a cut-off (Attack 7 is subtraction of a cut-off point from each; if result negative, zero). So it's a floor at zero. To get a max of 32.75, the cut-off point would have to be less than 7.38 (40.13-32.75). If cut-off is 7.38, then original 27.46 becomes 20.08, original 26.40 becomes 19.02, but we have values as low as 3.86. So impossible. Attack 7 is ruled out.

Thus, Attack 3 is the best match.

Now we need to output the classification and explanation.

Confidence: High. The evidence is strong: the drastic reduction in mean, the increased relative variability, the wide range of scaling factors when comparing to the reference day's pattern, and the preservation of some temporal structure but with random scaling per sample. Attack 3 is defined as exactly that.

Alternatives considered: Attack 1 and Attack 5. Attack 1 ruled out because scaling factor is not constant (ratios vary from 0.10 to 0.93). Attack 5 ruled out because there is still some correlation with the original pattern (peaks align partially), and Attack 5 would generate completely random values with no correlation.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Reasoning content 
We are given statistics for a reference week (7 days) and a query day (DAY_ID=6). The query day has metadata cal_month=12, dow=5, dom=27. The reference week also has a Day 6 with cal_month=12, dow=5, dom=6. So the query day is on the same weekday (Friday) but later in the month. The reference week's Day 6 has min=431.65, max=1718.24, std=433.77, mean=1185.58. The query day has min=136.71, max=1130.62, std=292.15, mean=689.74. This is a significant reduction in mean (about 58% of the reference day's mean). The max is lower, min is much lower. The consumption pattern is likely manipulated.

Observing the query day, there is a mix of high values (above 1000) and very low values (like 136.71, 166.43, 205.98, etc.). The pattern is erratic. The reference week Day 6 shows a typical daily pattern: morning low, then increase, afternoon peak, etc. The query day has many near-zero or very low values interspersed with high values. For example, the first reading is 1106.58 (high), then 440.82 (medium), then 621.35, etc., but later there are values like 220.66, 225.90, 275.34, 136.71, etc. Notably, there are many high spikes like 1106.58, 1107.76, 1032.51, 973.94, 1003.98, 1130.62, 1116.79, 1121.13, 1035.16, 1039.55, 1061.76, 1054.19, 1103.39, 1120.01, 1013.88, 1062.24, 1127.92, 1037.27, 973.35, 1032.73, 959.82, 1112.72. These highs are similar to the reference day's high values (ref day has max 1718.24, many in 1400-1700). But the query day highs are capped around 1130.62 max, which is lower than normal peak. Also the lows are extremely low, with many below 300. The reference day's minimum is 431.65, and most low night readings are around 400-500. So the query day has both suppressed peaks and severely reduced off-peak values.

Now, attack types:
Attack 0: all zeros. Not applicable, there are many nonzero values.
Attack 1: multiply by constant factor (0,1). If factor were constant, the pattern would be scaled down proportionally. But here the pattern shape is different: the low night values are much lower relative to the peak. For example, in ref Day 6, night values range ~430-500, peak ~1500-1700. In query, night values go down to 136, while peak ~1130. The ratio of peak to night in ref is about 3-4, in query it's about 8-9. So not a simple scaling. Also there is extra variability; some values are very low, some are moderate, but the highs are not scaled uniformly. Attack 1 would preserve the trend shape with reduced magnitude, but here the trend is disrupted with sudden drops.
Attack 2: on-off attack: zero for random duration. We see many low values (e.g., 136.71, 166.43, 205.98, 207.69, 214.02, 220.66, 225.90, 243.43, 273.16, 277.38, 285.91, 296.75, 300.21, 322.85, 325.30, etc.). Some are near zero but not exactly zero. Attack 2 replaces with zeros. But here none are exactly zero; min is 136.71. So not Attack 2, which would have zeros.
Attack 3: multiply by different random factors per sample. This could produce erratic pattern with varying reduction. The highs are roughly similar but reduced, lows are very low. Could be a possibility: each sample multiplied by random factor between 0 and 1. The original values from ref day would be scaled, but then the shape might still be somewhat similar but with added noise. This fits the erratic nature with some high values and many low ones.
Attack 4: randomly selected period, all measurements reduced. If a whole period is reduced, we would see a contiguous block of lower values. But the query day shows low values interspersed with high throughout the day. Not one contiguous block.
Attack 5: each sample replaced by false value = average normal consumption * random(0,1). Average normal consumption of ref week? The mean of ref Day 6 is 1185.58. If each sample is replaced by 1185.58 * random(0,1), the values would be anywhere from 0 to 1185.58, with no relation to time-of-day. The query day shows some structure: peaks in morning and later, but many lows. Could be Attack 5 if the attacker generated random values around a mean. But note that some values exceed 1100, close to the mean, and many are low. The pattern appears somewhat random but still has some intra-day variation: e.g., there are clusters of high values around indices 28-31 (1120, 1116, etc.) and later. However, it's not a smooth daily profile. The reference day has a clear morning low, afternoon peak. The query day has scattered highs and lows with no clear trend. This could be completely fake data (Attack 5). But Attack 5 says "each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample." That would generate completely random values around a mean, which might look like the query day, though with no correlation.
Attack 6: random cut-off, all above replaced by that cut-off value. The max is 1130.62, which is lower than ref day (1718). But we don't see a flat cutoff where many values are exactly at the cutoff point. The highs vary: 1106, 1130, 1120, etc., not a constant ceiling. So not Attack 6.
Attack 7: subtract cut-off, if negative report zero. This would lower both peaks and off-peaks, potentially creating zeros. The query day has no zeros, so not Attack 7.
Attack 9: all samples replaced by average daily energy consumption (flat). Not flat, so no.
Attack 10: reverses consumption pattern. The pattern doesn't look reversed (morning high, night low? Actually the query day has high at start (1106), then drops, then high again, etc. Not a clear reversal.)
Attack 12: replace with pattern of lower-consuming user. Could be, but we don't have another user pattern. The pattern is erratic, maybe from another household. But the statistics show a much lower mean and different std.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-min interval, either report actual or substitute with zero. Again, no zeros, so no. But it says "substitutes it with zero" – no zeros here. So not IEEE.

So the main candidates are Attack 3 (random scaling per sample) and Attack 5 (random values based on average). Also Attack 4 could be if the reduction period is the whole day but with varying reduction? Attack 4 says "all measurements in that period are reduced." Not specified how reduced, just reduced. Could be reduced by a factor or replaced. But it typically means a contiguous block. The whole day is one period? That would be Attack 1 if constant factor, but here it's varying. Attack 3 is precisely "actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample." That seems very plausible: each original value is scaled by a random factor, resulting in some near-normal and some very low. This would change both trend and amplitude.

Attack 5 would be replacing with a completely random pattern unrelated to actual profile. The query day's values seem somewhat structured: there are several high values clustered around certain times, mirroring perhaps the peaks of the original pattern but suppressed. For instance, original Day 6: peaks around mid-day and afternoon. Query day: we see high values like 1106 at start, then later 1130 around index 28, then clusters around index 50-60. The original Day 6: starts with 992, 1080, then down at night, then up to 1278, 1300, etc. The query day also has higher values in the morning (first reading 1106, then later 1107, etc.) and then scattered. It's not a clear preservation of the trend, but there might be some correlation. Given Attack 3 multiplies each sample by a random factor in (0,1), the resulting time series would have a shape that roughly follows the original shape but with multiplicative noise. That would sometimes produce very low values when the original is already low (night) multiplied by a small factor, and high values when original is high multiplied by a near-1 factor. This matches: night original low ~400-500, multiplied by e.g. 0.3 gives 120-150, which we see. Day original high ~1500, multiplied by ~0.7 gives ~1050, which we see. So Attack 3 fits well.

Attack 5 would generate values independent of time, so there would be no correlation with typical daily pattern; the high values could appear at night equally likely. But in the query day, the very low values (around 136, 166, etc.) occur at times when we might expect low consumption (night), and high values occur at times when we might expect high consumption (daytime). Let's check the timing: 96 values, each 15 min. The first reading (index 0) is midnight? Typically, day starts at 00:00. Reference Day 6 starts at 992.42 (presumably 00:00-00:15). That's high because it's winter, maybe heating at night? Actually in the reference Day 6, the first reading is 992, then 1080, then 915, 849, 816, then down to 596, 563, 552, etc. So nighttime early morning has moderate consumption, then drops to low during sleeping hours (around 2-4 AM). The query day's first reading is 1106.58 (high), then 440.82, 621.35, 732.37, etc., which is somewhat similar to the pattern but with more erratic drops. Later, around indices 24-30 (6-7:30 AM), reference Day 6 shows 970, 904, 1278, 1300 (a morning peak). Query day around indices 24-30: 136.71, 1003.98, 507.73, 296.75, 1130.62, 683.54, 1116.79, 1121.13. So there are some high values there, but also extremely low (136.71 at index 24?). Index 24 is 06:00-06:15. That is very low. So the pattern is disrupted. But overall, there might be some correlation with the original times. However, Attack 5 would produce completely random values independent of time, which might not align with expected high/low periods. Given the presence of many high values during typical peak times, Attack 3 seems more likely: the attacker used the actual consumption and multiplied each reading by a random factor, preserving the general shape (peaks and troughs at similar times) but with random reductions.

Also Attack 2 would be on/off with zeros, but no zeros. Attack IEEE explicitly says substitute with zero, no zeros. So eliminated.

Attack 1 is constant scaling, which we ruled out due to inconsistent scaling.

Attack 4 is a period reduction; if the period were the whole day but with different reductions per sample, that's essentially Attack 3. Attack 4 definition: "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced." It doesn't specify how they are reduced, could be by a constant factor or by zeroing, but typical NTL attacks like partial bypass might reduce by a factor during a period. The period might be a contiguous block. The query doesn't show a contiguous block of uniformly reduced values; it is interspersed. So not Attack 4.

Attack 5 would be a complete fabrication. The pattern might look completely different from normal profile. Our query day still has some structure reminiscent of a daily pattern: for example, the first few readings: 1106, 440, 621, 732, 835, 749, 912, 998, 1107... that is not entirely random; it goes high-low-medium etc. Could be random, but it's plausible to be a distorted original.

Given the data, Attack 3 seems the most likely: each sample multiplied by a different random factor in (0,1). That explains why some values are near original (when factor ~1) and some are very low (when factor small). The overall mean is reduced, std is reduced (292 vs 433), max is also reduced (1130 vs 1718). Trend is altered due to the random factors, but the underlying daily pattern is still vaguely present.

Alternatives: Attack 12 (pattern swapping) would produce a different pattern, possibly with a lower mean and different daily shape, but we can't verify without another user. Attack 5 would generate completely random values, which may not exhibit the slight temporal correlation seen. Attack 2/IEEE ruled out due to no zeros.

Thus, classification: Manipulated. Most likely: Attack 3. Confidence: Medium, as Attack 5 is also plausible, but Attack 3 better matches the preservation of some high values at times consistent with original peaks.

This means some readings remain close to the original (factor near 1) while others are drastically reduced (factor near 0), creating a mix of moderately high and extremely low values.
- Impact on magnitude and trend: The overall magnitude is strongly reduced (average roughly halved). The underlying consumption trend is still faintly visible (e.g., higher values during daytime), but the per‑sample random scaling makes the profile appear highly irregular and less smooth than the normal week.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Reasoning content 
We are given a reference week of energy consumption for a smart meter (7 days, each with 96 readings at 15-min intervals) and a query day (DAY_ID=1) which corresponds to a Monday (dow=0) but in a different month (cal_month=6 vs. reference week cal_month=5 for the same DAY_ID=1). The query day data is provided: 96 readings. We need to determine if the query day is original or manipulated, and if manipulated, identify the attack type.

First, let's analyze the reference week statistics and patterns.

Reference week (Days 0 to 6, all in cal_month=5, days of week: Day0=Sunday? dow=6 is Saturday? Wait: dow: 0=Mon, 1=Tue, 2=Wed, 3=Thu, 4=Fri, 5=Sat, 6=Sun? In the metadata: dow=0..6 Mon-Sun. So 0=Monday, 1=Tuesday, 2=Wednesday, 3=Thursday, 4=Friday, 5=Saturday, 6=Sunday.

Reference week:
Day 0: cal_month=5, dow=6, dom=0 -> Sunday (dow=6)
Day 1: cal_month=5, dow=0, dom=1 -> Monday (dow=0)
Day 2: cal_month=5, dow=1, dom=2 -> Tuesday
Day 3: cal_month=5, dow=2, dom=3 -> Wednesday
Day 4: cal_month=5, dow=3, dom=4 -> Thursday
Day 5: cal_month=5, dow=4, dom=5 -> Friday
Day 6: cal_month=5, dow=5, dom=6 -> Saturday

Query day: DAY_ID=1, cal_month=6, dow=0, dom=22 -> Monday, but in June.

So the query day is a Monday, and the reference week has a Monday (Day1). So we can compare the query day's pattern with the reference week's Monday pattern, and also with the overall week behavior.

Let's compute the statistics for the query day: min=14.77, max=103.38, std=23.70, mean=54.15. Compare with reference week days:
Day1 (Monday in reference week): min=53.16, max=157.71, std=30.48, mean=113.01.
The query day's mean is about half (54.15 vs 113.01), min is much lower (14.77 vs 53.16), max is lower (103.38 vs 157.71). This suggests a significant reduction. The standard deviation is slightly lower but not drastically different relative to mean.

Looking at the query day values: many are very low compared to typical Monday values. For example, reference Monday has many values above 100, even peaks around 150. Query day max is only 103.38 and there are many low values like 14.77, 15.57, 15.99, etc. There are also some values around 90s, but overall magnitude is reduced.

Now, consider the shape of consumption. In reference Monday (Day1), we can see the typical daily pattern: low overnight, morning ramp-up, high during day/evening, then decline. Let's check query day: values fluctuate with some low (teens, 20s, 30s) and some moderate (70s, 80s, 90s, one 103.38). The pattern seems irregular: there are some zero-like lows but not truly zeros, just very low. For example, 14.77, 17.42, 23.96, 18.30, 14.77, etc. These are far lower than any reference week low. In reference Monday, the lowest is 53.16; in other days, lowest is 53.16 (Day0), 54.41 (Day2), 55.65 (Day3), 54.41 (Day4), 56.9 (Day5), 58.14 (Day6). So the minimum across the week is 53.16. The query day has min 14.77, which is way below normal.

Also, query day has many values that look like they might be random scaling? Let's see if the pattern roughly follows the typical Monday shape but scaled down. Compare the sequence: Reference Monday Day1 values (first few): 92.04, 87.04, 63.14, 93.29, 73.10, 70.61, 79.54, 53.16, ... Query day: 82.63, 75.74, 36.75, 52.56, 57.76, 93.93, 71.76, 44.91, ... Not obviously a simple scaling: 82.63 vs 92.04 (roughly 0.9), 75.74 vs 87.04 (0.87), 36.75 vs 63.14 (0.58), then 52.56 vs 93.29 (0.56), 57.76 vs 73.10 (0.79), 93.93 vs 70.61 (1.33) - actually higher. So not consistent scaling factor.

The query day values appear highly variable and not a simple constant reduction. They seem to be a mix of some normal-level values and very low values, almost like some readings are heavily reduced while others remain closer to normal? For example, 93.93 is close to typical morning values, 103.38 is high but still below typical peak (150+). 82.63 is close to normal. But then 36.75 is very low for that time. This looks like an attack where some readings are reduced or zeroed out, but not all.

Let's check the definition of Attack IEEE: "For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day." In the query day, many values are very low but not exactly zero. They are low (e.g., 14.77, 15.57, 15.99, 16.43, etc.), but not zero. Attack 2 is zeros for random duration (on-off), which also creates zeros. But here zeros are not present; instead, very low values. That could be a scaling variation? Attack 4: "reduces over a randomly selected period" - but the reduction is not a zero; it's some reduction. Attack 1 multiplies by a constant random factor between 0 and 1; that would give a reduced profile but all values would be scaled by the same factor. Here the factor seems to vary. Attack 3 multiplies each sample by a different random factor between 0 and 1. That could produce a pattern where some readings are heavily reduced and some mildly reduced. That matches: we see some readings like 14.77 (very low factor), 93.93 (factor close to 1? compared to reference Monday, perhaps normal values around that time could be ~90-100; 93.93 might be actual), and 103.38 (maybe actual peak is around 150? factor ~0.7). Attack 5 replaces each sample by average*random variable; that would produce values unrelated to the actual pattern. Here the values still seem to have some correlation with typical times? For example, morning 82.63, 75.74 could be normal. But then 36.75 at quarter hour? Not sure.

This seems closest to Attack 3: "The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading." That would produce a pattern where some readings are close to original (factor near 1) and some are heavily reduced (factor near 0). Here, some readings like 93.93, 95.10, 103.38, 94.54, 95.65 could be near original if the original was say 100-150, factor ~0.7-0.9. While low readings like 14.77 might be original ~60-70 scaled by ~0.25.

Alternatively, Attack IEEE: zero or actual. But zeros are not present; we see values like 14.77 not zero. So that's not it.

Attack 5 replaces with random value based on average; that would produce a flatter profile. Here we still see some diurnal variation (higher during day, lower at night) but with erratic low spikes. The pattern roughly resembles a typical consumption pattern with occasional huge drops. For instance, from index 0-7: 82.63,75.74,36.75,52.56,57.76,93.93,71.76,44.91. The 36.75 is low but not near zero; the rest follow a typical morning ramp. At index 17: 17.42 is very low. Index 21: 14.77. So it's like many readings were hit with a very small multiplier.

Attack 6 (cut-off) would produce a pattern where no value exceeds a certain threshold; here max is 103.38, but normal peak was 157.71. So it could be a cut-off around 103.38? But then values above that are replaced by the cut-off, meaning many values would be exactly 103.38. We see only one 103.38; others are below. So not a constant cut-off.

Attack 7: subtract a cut-off, so all values reduced by constant, with floor at 0. That would reduce all values, but the lowest values would become zero. Here we don't see zeros, but the min is 14.77, so if original min was 53.16, subtracting ~38 would give 14.77, but then many values would become negative and be reported as zero. So we would see many zeros. Not this.

Attack 2: zeros for random duration. Not zeros.

Attack 4: "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced." That could produce a segment of reduced values. Looking at the query day, is there a contiguous period where values are reduced? The low values appear scattered throughout the day, not in a single contiguous block. For example, low values at index 9 (29.46), 14 (36.64), 17 (17.42), 19-21 (23.96,18.30,14.77), 27 (25.76), 32 (27.85), 35-37 (23.23,28.42,16.43), 49 (15.57), 54 (22.38), 58 (15.99), 60 (22.10), 71 (33.15), 74 (31.45), 76 (27.63), 88-89 (24.01,25.42), 94 (23.72). They are scattered. So not a single contiguous period.

Attack 12: replace with lower-consuming user's pattern. That would produce a pattern that resembles some other typical user but not this user; the shape might be different but still realistic. Here the shape is irregular with deep drops that don't look like typical consumption (e.g., a sudden drop to 14.77 at index 21, then back to 54.52 at index 22). That is not realistic normal consumption.

Attack 10: reverses pattern, no reduction. Not here because total consumption is reduced (mean is halved).

So, the most plausible is Attack 3: each sample multiplied by a different random factor in (0,1). That explains both the variation in magnitude and the fact that the overall shape is somewhat preserved but distorted due to random scaling per sample.

Why not Attack 5? Attack 5 would produce values that are completely random based on average, so there would be little correlation with the typical pattern. Here, there is still some correlation: e.g., morning hours (index 0-8) show values 82.63,75.74,36.75,52.56,57.76,93.93,71.76,44.91,87.62. The 93.93 and 87.62 could be normal for that time, while 36.75 is low. So it's like the pattern is present but heavily distorted. Attack 5 would replace with random numbers around mean (which is ~54), so values would cluster around 50-60, not have high 90s and low teens simultaneously. For reference, the average normal consumption for Monday is 113, but Attack 5 defines "multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1" where average normal consumption likely refers to the overall mean or daily mean. That would produce values uniformly distributed between 0 and ~113, so we'd see many values around 50-60 on average, but the max would be up to 113. Actually, we do see max 103.38, min 14.77, so that range matches possible uniform distribution. But the pattern in Attack 5 would be purely random noise, no time-of-day pattern. The query day still shows some diurnal pattern: early morning (0-8) mostly 75-93 with a dip; midday (indices 24-48) some lower values but also some spikes; evening (indices 60-95) some higher values (70s-90s). It's not completely random; there is some structure. So Attack 5 is less likely.

Attack 1 would be a single constant factor, so the entire curve would be scaled uniformly. The ratio of each query value to the reference Monday value would be roughly constant. Let's test that quickly: Reference Monday Day1 values (first 10): 92.04, 87.04, 63.14, 93.29, 73.10, 70.61, 79.54, 53.16, 71.86, 70.61. Query: 82.63, 75.74, 36.75, 52.56, 57.76, 93.93, 71.76, 44.91, 87.62, 29.46. Ratios: 0.898, 0.87, 0.58, 0.56, 0.79, 1.33 (wait 93.93/70.61=1.33, but if original was 92.04? However, for attack 1, the factor would be constant across all samples in that day. The ratios vary wildly (0.56 to 1.33). That's not constant. So Attack 1 is ruled out.

Attack IEEE is zero or actual, but no zeros present. However, could the "zero" be interpreted as very low due to some noise? The definition says "substitutes it with zero." So we'd expect exact zeros. Not the case.

Attack 4 is period-specific reduction. The low values are scattered, not in a single period. So ruled out.

Attack 6: cut-off max, but we'd see many exactly at the cut-off. Not present.

Attack 7: subtract constant, would produce zeros. Not.

Thus, Attack 3 is the most likely: each sample multiplied by a different random factor.

Confidence: Medium, because the pattern is consistent with Attack 3, but there's also possibility of Attack 5 if the apparent diurnal structure is coincidental. However, given the presence of some high values near expected times and low values at other times, it's more like variable scaling than complete replacement.

Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

Overall, the normal days have means around 390-405, max around 500-506, min around 180-225. So typical consumption is moderate, with a daily pattern that varies from ~200 at night to ~500 during peak.

Query day: min=42.98, max=378.13, std=97.39, mean=225.32

Mean is drastically reduced: 225 vs ~394 (reference Day 6 mean 396.93). Max is also drastically reduced: 378 vs ~504. Min is also lower: 42.98 vs ~188. The standard deviation is similar (~97 vs ~99), but that could be misleading. So significant reduction in overall magnitude.

Now look at the query day readings: 
The values fluctuate wildly, with many very low values (e.g., 42.98, 46.31, 51.65, 51.74, 52.01, 55.25, etc.) and some moderate values up to 378.13. The pattern appears to have many gaps where consumption drops to very low numbers (like 42-150) interspersed with higher values around 300-378. In the reference week, the typical pattern for Day 6 (a Friday) shows a gradual increase from morning (around 200s) to afternoon peaks (around high 400s-500), then evening decrease. The query day does not follow that pattern: it starts at 172.21 (lower than normal), then spikes to 374.52, then drops to 71.66, then 173.25, etc. There are many intervals with very low consumption (<100) which never occur in normal days (normal min is around 180). This looks like an on-off attack or random zero-like reductions.

Let's examine the distribution of query day values. The query day has 96 readings. Sorting them: 
42.98, 46.31, 51.65, 51.74, 52.01, 55.25, 71.66, 77.43, 85.37, 86.40, 91.11, 101.58, 103.24, 116.39, 117.65, 121.14, 124.65, 125.85, 135.88, 137.99, 144.17, 144.72, 146.12, 151.08, 158.71, 164.15, 164.74, 165.56, 165.70, 167.89, 170.08, 172.21, 173.25, 173.75, 176.20, 178.44, 178.64, 183.69, 187.19, 199.46, 200.54, 202.79, 203.12, 206.40, 208.15, 211.48, 220.50, 221.64, 223.74, 225.95, 227.55, 232.25, 236.29, 237.46, 239.42, 241.97, 249.57, 264.12, 264.43, 265.82, 265.84, 268.91, 269.66, 270.34, 274.61, 276.36, 282.96, 286.67, 292.77, 305.18, 306.07, 314.08, 315.59, 319.66, 320.04, 325.21, 329.13, 331.11, 343.71, 346.18, 346.91, 348.16, 355.04, 355.15, 358.69, 359.05, 362.47, 362.96, 365.15, 369.94, 373.95, 374.52, 376.52, 377.34, 377.65, 378.13.

So about half the values are below 200, with many in the 40-150 range. This is not a simple scaling factor because the higher values are around 370, which is not proportionally scaled from 500 (500 scaled by something would be around 500 * (225/396) ≈ 284, not 370). In fact, the max of query day (378) is closer to the normal max but still reduced. The pattern appears to have some intervals where consumption is relatively normal (300-378) and others where it's drastically cut (40-150). This suggests an on-off attack where some intervals are zeroed or heavily reduced, while others remain normal or slightly reduced.

Attack 2: "Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack." However, here the values are not exactly zero but some are very low (42-50). Attack 2 says "replacing with zeros", but the description also includes "changes both trend and amplitude." Could it be Attack 4? "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced." But Attack 4 reduces by a constant factor? It says "all measurements in that period are reduced" but doesn't specify how. Could be a constant factor or random per sample. Attack IEEE: "For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero." That matches the pattern of some intervals being zero (or near zero) and others normal. But the query day's low values are not exactly zero; they are small positive numbers like 42.98, 71.66, etc. That could be a partial reduction, not zero. Attack 2 replaces with zeros, not small values. Attack 3: multiplies by different random factors between 0 and 1 per sample. That would result in reduced values that are proportionally scaled from original, but not zero. For example, if an original 500 is multiplied by a random factor, it could become 250, 100, etc., but also small values like 200 could become 40. So Attack 3 could explain the mix of drastically reduced low values and moderately high values. Attack 1 multiplies by a single constant factor, which would uniformly scale all values. The query day does not show uniform scaling because the ratio of max to min is not consistent: normal max~500 reduced to 378 (factor ~0.75), normal min~188 reduced to 42 (factor ~0.22). So factor varies per sample. So Attack 1 is ruled out. Attack 5: each sample replaced by false value = average normal consumption * random between 0 and 1. That would produce values around average (225 if average normal is 390, random *390 yields 0-390). That could yield some high values near 390 and low near 0. That might also match. Attack 6: random cut-off point replaces values above with cutoff value. That would result in many values exactly equal to the cutoff. The query day does not have a repeated constant cutoff value; many values are distinct, so not Attack 6 or 7. Attack 7 subtracts cutoff, resulting in zeros for some. Could produce very low values but not a varied low range. Attack 9: all samples replaced by constant average -> flat, not the case. Attack 10: reverses pattern, not causing magnitude reduction. Attack 12: replaced with lower consumer's pattern, would have a different pattern but magnitude reduced, could have similar variability. Attack IEEE: replaces with zero for some intervals and actual for others. That would create true zeros. Here we have no zeros, just very low values (min 42.98). So it's unlikely to be true zero replacement. Could be a very low baseline (like magnetic interference scaling factor near zero for some intervals). Attack 3 multiplies by random factor per sample, and if factor is close to zero, you get near-zero values. That matches.

Also note that for Attack 3, each sample has its own random factor between 0 and 1. That would cause both the trend and amplitude to change because the relative proportions between samples are altered. The query day's pattern is indeed altered: the typical daily trend (low at night, high during day) is disrupted with many low values scattered. So Attack 3 seems plausible.

But we must consider Attack 4: "Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced." That implies a contiguous block where values are reduced. Looking at the query day, there are low values throughout: e.g., first few: 172.21 (moderate), 374.52 (high), 71.66 (low), 173.25 (moderate), 178.44 (moderate), 85.37 (low), 86.40 (low), 348.16 (high), 42.98 (very low)... The low values are not concentrated in a single contiguous block; they appear interleaved with moderate/high values across the entire day. So it's not a single contiguous period; it's more like random individual samples reduced. Therefore Attack 4 is less likely.

Attack 2 is on-off with zeros, but again we have no zeros and pattern is not contiguous zeros. Attack IEEE is similar but also zeros.

So Attack 3: multiplying each sample by a different random factor, seems best fit. The factors could be anything from ~0.08 (for the 42 value) to ~0.75 (for the 378). This matches the description of "changes both trend and amplitude" and "unique factor per sample". Technical causes: meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current.

Classification: Manipulated

Identified Attack:  
- Most Likely: Attack 3  
- Confidence: High  
- Reasoning:  
  - Attack 3 matches because the query day’s consumption values are all significantly reduced compared to the reference week, yet the reduction is not uniform. The ratio max/min increases from ~4.4 (typical 1470/333) to ~8.9 (757/84), indicating that low values were scaled down more aggressively than high values. This is consistent with per-sample multiplication by random factors in (0,1).  
  - Attack 1 (constant factor) was ruled out because a constant scaling would preserve the max/min ratio and the overall shape of the normal day, whereas the query day shows a distorted pattern with some moderate peaks but many extremely low dips, inconsistent with a single multiplier.  
  - Attack 5 (random values around the daily mean) was ruled out because the query day exhibits temporal clustering (groups of moderate values and groups of low values) and serial correlation, not pure white noise. The series does not resemble a completely independent, flat random process.

Attack_Behavior_Explanation:  
- How consumption values are manipulated relative to the normal week:  
  Each original 15‑minute reading is multiplied by a different random factor between 0 and 1. This means every sample is individually scaled down, but by varying degrees. For instance, a normal peak of ~1400 W may be reduced to ~700 W (factor ~0.5), while a normal low of ~350 W may be reduced to ~85 W (factor ~0.24).  
- Impact on magnitude and trend:  
  Magnitude is globally reduced, but unevenly, so the overall consumption level is about half the normal mean. The trend is disrupted because the per‑sample multipliers distort the original consumption shape – peaks may be muted, and low periods may become even lower, creating an erratic, noisy profile that still loosely follows the original daily rhythm.  
- Possible technical causes:  
  This attack can be realized by magnetic interference, reverse current, or certain types of false data injection where the attacker cannot keep a constant bypass (e.g., fluctuations in the tampering device’s effectiveness) or deliberately varies the reduction to avoid simple proportionality checks.
Prompt 
You are a domain expert in smart grid security and electricity theft analysis, with deep knowledge of non-technical loss (NTL) attack patterns in smart meters.
You are given:
1. A plot of one week of original (normal) energy consumption for a smart meter, provided as context for the customer's typical consumption behavior.
2. A plot of one day of energy consumption for the same smart meter, which may or may not be manipulated.
Below are common attack types, each defined by how consumption values are altered, their impact on magnitude and trend, and their possible technical causes.
Attack 0: Generated by replacing all electricity consumption samples with zero values for the entire observation duration. Simulates a scenario where no energy usage is reported at all. May be caused by a total meter bypass or severe meter malfunction. Completely eliminates both the trend and magnitude of the consumption profile.
Attack 1: Multiplies the actual energy consumption by a constant random value between (0,1). The lower the factor, the higher the severity. Can be triggered by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. The consumption trend remains the same but the magnitude is uniformly reduced.
Attack 2: Generated by replacing consumption samples with zeros for a random duration. Also known as an on-off attack. Can be caused by full meter bypass, false data injection, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 3: The actual consumption is multiplied by different random factors between 0 and 1, with a unique factor per sample. Similar to Attack 1 but the scaling factor varies per reading. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, and reverse current. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 4: Simulates energy theft over a randomly selected period within the observation window. All measurements in that period are reduced. Caused by meter bypass, magnetic interference, reverse current, and false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 5: Each sample is replaced by a false value obtained by multiplying the average normal consumption by a random variable between 0 and 1, with a different variable per sample. Caused by false data injection. Produces a completely different pattern from the original consumption profile, changing both trend and magnitude.
Attack 6: A random cut-off point is selected and all consumption values above it are replaced by that cut-off value. The attacker avoids exceeding a predefined maximum limit. Mainly caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.
Attack 7: A random cut-off point is subtracted from each sample; if the result is negative, zero is reported. Similar to Attack 6 but replaces exceeding values with zero instead of the cut-off point. May be caused by false data injection or total meter bypass. Does not change the trend but reduces overall consumption.
Attack 9: All samples are replaced by the average daily energy consumption, resulting in a flat constant profile. Easily detectable because constant consumption does not reflect normal customer behavior. Caused by false data injection. Eliminates all trends.
Attack 10: Reverses the consumption pattern without reducing the total amount. Applicable when the electricity company uses time-varying pricing, allowing the attacker to shift high consumption to off-peak periods. Caused by false data injection. Changes the trend without decreasing total consumption.
Attack 12: The attacker replaces their consumption pattern with that of a lower-consuming user. The legitimate user unknowingly pays the adversary's electricity bill. Caused by false data injection.
Attack IEEE: For each 15-minute interval across the entire day, the consumer either reports the actual electricity consumption or substitutes it with zero. The result is a profile where some intervals reflect real consumption while others are zeroed out, creating an irregular pattern across the full day. May be caused by false data injection. Changes both the trend and amplitude.

Given the two plots, determine:
1. Whether the day is attacked or original - compare the flagged day against the normal week and assess whether the consumption pattern is consistent with normal behavior or shows signs of manipulation. If the day is consistent with the customer's typical consumption behavior, classify it as original and provide no further analysis. If manipulation is detected, proceed to point 2.
2. Which attack type this most likely is - identify the single most probable attack and justify your choice based on how the flagged day deviates from the normal week.
3. The behavior of the identified attack - explain how the consumption values are manipulated, their impact on magnitude and trend, and the possible technical causes behind this type of attack.

The reference week shows consistent high peaks in the afternoon/evening (e.g., around 1400s). The query day pattern seems to have a reduced magnitude overall, but some intervals still show moderate consumption (700s). The pattern is irregular, with some zeros? No, min is 84.91, not zero. There is no flat zero period. The values are mostly between 100 and 700, much lower than normal.

- Attack 0: all zeros. Not this, because values are not zero.
- Attack 1: multiply by constant factor between 0 and 1. This would preserve the shape (trend) but scale uniformly. - Attack 2: replacing with zeros for random duration. There are no explicit zero values, but there are very low values like 84.91, 94.73, 101.12, which could be considered near-zero? But they are not zero. Attack 2 specifically zeros. So unlikely.

The query day shows erratic values with some high spikes and very low dips. This could be consistent with random per-sample scaling. The overall magnitude is reduced, but not uniformly. This seems plausible.

So if the period is the whole day, then all values are reduced. But how are they reduced? Multiplied by a constant? Not specified. Typically it might be a constant factor. The query day pattern doesn't seem to be a constant reduction from any reference day, because the reference day has a steady peak pattern, while the query day is noisy with many low and high values. So Attack 4 is less likely.
